Design Note — XP Balance: 6,125 XP from all 66 quests represents roughly 26% of the 24,000 XP needed for Master Beekeeper. The farewell, holiday, and community quests deepen the emotional and social fabric without inflating progression — their XP rewards are deliberately modest compared to the core chains. NPC quests provide structure and narrative. The bees provide the progression.
Design Note — Failure as Progression: Quest failures don't lock the player out permanently. A failed seasonal goal delays progression by one season and costs standing, but the quest reappears. NPC quest failures delay the chain by one season and reduce standing. The design ensures a path forward always exists — just a slower one. This mirrors real beekeeping: a bad year sets you back, but it doesn't end you.

09

9. NPCs

NPCs are not decorative — each serves a functional role in the player's progression and provides authentic beekeeping context through their dialogue.

NPCRoleRelationship Arc
Uncle Bob (Home Property NPC)Vietnam veteran, early 60s. The player's uncle. Experienced beekeeper, 20 years. Lives alone since wife Sandy died eight years ago. Liver failure from Agent Orange exposure -- the war's slow-burning consequence. Implemented in scripts/npc/uncle_bob.gd. Stands at position (380, 140) on the home property. Interact via E key. Connections: Built Darlene's first nuc box. He and Lloyd hunted mushrooms every fall for 30 years. Rose saves him pecan pie every Tuesday (Sandy's order). Argued with Paul Rowland about pesticides in 2019.Year 1: Tutorial anchor and mentor. 8-quest mentorship chain doubles as the extended tutorial. Appears at the Crossroads Diner on Tuesdays. Year 2-3: Recurring mentorship visits. Health visibly declining. Year 4-5: Farewell quest chain (Q9-Q12) -- Bob puts his affairs in order. Year 5: Death (liver failure). Telling the Bees. Memorial service. The war took forty years to finish what it started.
Darlene Kowalski (Mentor Beekeeper)Retired master beekeeper, late 60s. Lives on the adjacent property. Third-generation Cedar Bend beekeeper. Warm, practical, no-nonsense. Connections: Bob built her first nuc box when she was starting out (she still has it). Brings sour cream raisin pie to every funeral for 40 years. Brings the Telling the Bees tradition to the player when Bob dies. She and Rose are the two pillars of Cedar Bend's social structure.Year 1: Teacher and safety net. Year 2: Peer. Year 3+: The player is now the expert and she defers to them on new techniques. After Bob's death: She is the one who tells the player about "Telling the Bees" -- the emotional climax of the game.
Frank Fischbach (Market Vendor)Runs the Cedar Bend Saturday Market. Sells everything from sweet corn to pickles. Primary buyer for all player honey products. Connections: Plays cribbage with Carl every Wednesday at the diner. Offers the player a premium booth at Founder's Beam. Handles county fair honey competition entries.Relationship improves with consistent quality and reliability. Unlocks premium shelf space, bulk orders, and county fair invitations.
Dr. Ellen Harwick (Extension Agent)County extension agent for Millhaven County, PhD from the University of Iowa (not Iowa State — she will correct you). Expert in integrated pest management and Midwest apiculture. Moved to Cedar Bend from Iowa City. Precise, data-driven, occasionally impatient with anecdotal claims. The Iowa/Iowa State rivalry with Kacey Rowland is the one thing that cracks her professional composure. Connections: Provides lab analysis for the player's hives. Her sentinel hive program (Q4) contributes regional data. Appears at Bob's memorial — sits in the back, stays after everyone else leaves.Triggered by pest or disease events. Provides lab analysis, treatment guidance, and the game's most technically demanding mastery quests. Her 5-quest chain gates the Master Beekeeper certification — the highest achievement in the game.
The Rowland Farm (Paul & Kacey)Family corn/soybean operation on the east side. Paul Rowland rotates crops annually — corn, soybean, sometimes oats. Big man, big voice, big opinions. The kind of farmer who measures a person by their handshake and whether they look him in the eye. Intimidating on first meeting — but behind the bluster is a man who once drove forty minutes to fix a widow's toilet at 2 AM and never mentioned it to anyone. Kacey found out from the widow's daughter. His daughter Kacey studied agronomy at Iowa State and is more open to sustainable practices. Kacey needles Dr. Ellen Harwick about Iowa State vs. University of Iowa — it is the only thing that makes Ellen visibly annoyed, and Kacey knows it. Connections: Argued with Bob about pesticides in 2019. Kacey bridges conventional farming and pollinator-friendly practices. Paul's "12% speech" at the market is his version of admitting he was wrong.Paul is initially dismissive — "I've got 600 acres to worry about. Your bees are your problem." Through quests with Kacey, the farm shifts toward cover crops and better pesticide timing, unlocking the Rowland Farm / Orchard Apiary. Paul comes around in Year 4 when crop yields prove him wrong. His quest chain is about earning trust through results, not arguments.
The Cedar Valley Beekeepers AssociationRegional beekeeping association. Meets monthly at the Grange Hall in Cedar Bend.Unlocked at Level 3. Offers queen trades, used equipment, and rare knowledge in exchange for reputation and honey donations.
Silas Crenshaw (Carpenter)Semi-retired carpenter and handyman, early 70s. Runs a small workshop behind the Cedar Bend hardware store. Built half the barns in Millhaven County. Quiet, precise, opinionated about lumber quality. Knew Uncle Bob well -- built the original Honey House for him decades ago. Takes pride in his work and expects the same from others.Year 1: Introduced through the dilapidated Honey House. Assesses the damage and agrees to rebuild if the player gathers materials and pays his labor fee. Year 1-2: Restores the Honey House through a 4-quest chain. Available afterward for structural upgrades and cosmetic building projects on the home property. His dialogue reflects a craftsman's philosophy -- he sees beekeeping as another form of building something that lasts. Connections: Built the original Honey House for Bob decades ago. Built Lloyd's timber shed. Works with Carl on supply chain for construction materials.
Carl Tanner (Feed & Supply Owner)Runs Tanner's Feed & Supply, the only farm supply store in Cedar Bend. Black man, early 50s, stocky build, dark skin weathered from years of loading docks and farm visits, short cropped hair graying at the temples, always in a canvas apron over a flannel shirt. Third-generation store owner -- his grandfather opened Tanner's in 1952, one of the first Black-owned businesses in Millhaven County. Knows every farmer in the county by name and what they ordered last spring. Practical, fair-priced, and genuinely invested in seeing local operations succeed. Keeps a well-organized bulletin board by the front door that serves as the town's unofficial information hub. His winter catalog is legendary among local farmers -- hand-annotated with his own recommendations.Year 1: Primary equipment and supply vendor. Sells hive components, feeding supplies, jars, tools, and seasonal stock. The store interior changes with the seasons. Year 1-2: Carl begins offering advice on equipment choices based on the player's operation size. Year 2+: Unlocks the winter specialty catalog (pre-order rare seeds, specialty equipment). His quest chain focuses on building a wholesale supply relationship and eventually stocking the player's honey on Tanner's shelves alongside the big brands. Connections: Plays cribbage with Frank every Wednesday at the diner. Judges 4-H at the county fair. Posts the bulletin board notice at Bob's memorial: "Robert Baker. Beekeeper. Vietnam Veteran. 1951-2031. The hives are still humming."
Rose Delacroix (Diner Owner)Owner and head cook of the Crossroads Diner, Cedar Bend's only restaurant. Late 40s, strong arms from decades of kitchen work, dark hair pinned back, always moving. Bought the diner from the previous owner fifteen years ago when nobody else would. Born in Cedar Bend, left for culinary school in Winston-Salem, North Carolina, came back when her mother got sick and never left again. The diner is her life's work and the town's living room. She employs Agnes as her waitress and keeps the place running from 6 AM to 9 PM six days a week. Closed Sundays -- "Even the Lord rested." Connections: Saves a slice of pecan pie for Bob every Tuesday (Sandy's order -- she never stopped). Coordinates the casserole circuit when someone is in need. She and Darlene are the twin pillars of Cedar Bend's community life. Calls the player "honey" regardless of character creation choices. Hosts Bob's memorial reception (pecan pie, no charge).Year 1: The diner is a functional energy recovery location and social hub. Rose is behind the counter or in the kitchen during all open hours. She knows everyone's business and shares it freely -- in a helpful way, not a gossipy one. Year 1-2: Rose begins commenting on the player's honey after tasting it. Year 2+: Rose's quest chain centers on sourcing local ingredients for her menu -- she wants the player's honey for her signature dishes and eventually for a line of house-made products (honey butter, honey-glazed ham, pie sweetened with local honey). The relationship arc transforms her from a service NPC into a business partner and community anchor.
June Wellman (Postmaster)Cedar Bend's postmaster. Asian American woman, late 20s, dark hair pulled back neatly, reading glasses perched on her nose. Took over the post office two years ago from her grandmother, Mae Wellman, who ran it for 31 years before retiring. June grew up sorting mail after school and knows every address in the county by heart -- not from decades of service but from a childhood spent in the back room while Grandma Mae worked the counter. Quiet, observant, precise with paperwork, and unexpectedly dry-humored for her age. The post office is the logistical backbone of rural life -- package bees arrive in spring through her building, and the buzzing boxes are a tradition she inherited and tolerates with the same professional calm her grandmother did. She sees everything that comes and goes and occasionally offers observations that reveal she pays closer attention to the town's rhythms than anyone realizes. Some of the older farmers still call her "little Mae." Connections: At Bob's memorial, delivers a photograph from her desk drawer -- Bob and Sandy in the apiary, young, laughing at something the camera didn't catch. She had been holding it for years, waiting for the right moment.Year 1: Functional NPC for receiving mail-order packages (bee packages, catalog orders from River Valley Bee Supply, specialty items). June hands over packages and confirms shipments. Year 1-2: June begins making small comments about what she has seen come through the mail -- "Third queen shipment this month. Somebody's building an operation." Year 2+: Her quest chain involves the postal service threatening to close the Cedar Bend branch. The player helps demonstrate the office's necessity to the community -- a storyline that touches June personally, since the post office is her grandmother's legacy as much as it is the town's infrastructure.
Lloyd Petersen (Woodlot Manager)Owner and steward of 80 acres of mixed hardwood timber east of Cedar Bend. Late 60s, lean, deliberate in speech and movement. Wears the same canvas jacket year-round. A lifelong woodsman and former county forester who retired early to manage his own land on his own terms. Knew Uncle Bob for decades -- they hunted mushrooms together in the timber every fall. Lloyd's land contains the linden grove and basswood canopy that make the Timber & Woodlot the most valuable mid-game apiary location. He is protective of his trees and skeptical of anyone who does not understand that a forest is a living system, not a resource to be extracted. Once trust is earned, he is generous with access and knowledge. Connections: Bob's oldest friend -- 30 years of mushroom hunting. Silas built Lloyd's timber shed. Tells the grandfather's bee story at the Long Table (Telling the Bees foreshadowing). At Bob's memorial, walks the timber alone and comes back with something to say.Year 1: Mentioned by Uncle Bob during Q6 (Battening Down) as "my friend Lloyd." Not directly interactable until Year 2. Year 2: Introduced through Bob Q7 (Lloyd's Lot). The player visits the timber with Bob. Lloyd agrees to allow apiary placement if the player pays a $200 clearing fee and demonstrates respect for the land. Year 2+: Lloyd's quest chain deepens the player's relationship with the woodlot -- tree identification, forage calendar mapping, sustainable lumber harvesting, and eventually becoming a co-steward of the timber. His dialogue reflects a conservationist philosophy rooted in practical experience rather than ideology.
Agnes Pfeiffer (Waitress)Rose's waitress at the Crossroads Diner. Early 20s. Present every time the player visits the diner — always pouring coffee, always quiet. Taking night classes at the community college in Waterloo, driving 45 minutes each way three nights a week after a full shift. Studying business administration. Grew up in Cedar Bend, father left when she was ten, mother works at the grain elevator. Rose hired her at sixteen. She loves Rose, loves the diner, and knows that if she doesn't leave, she'll be pouring coffee at sixty-five. Connections: Rose's mentee and surrogate daughter. Agnes serves Carl and Frank's Wednesday cribbage game without being asked.Year 1-2: Background NPC, always present at the diner. Year 2: The Study Break — player finds Agnes studying after close. She's embarrassed. "Don't tell Rose." Generational mirror to the player's own journey. Year 3: The Ride to Waterloo — Agnes's car breaks down on class night. Player drives her. The game's second sustained car-ride conversation. Year 4: The Application — Agnes asks for help with her UNI scholarship essay. Rose finds out Agnes is leaving. "You go. You go and you do it right." Year 5+: If Agnes leaves, Rose hires a new waitress. The diner feels slightly different — a tiny, true detail about how towns change.
Bandit (Bob's Cat)Gray tabby with a black mask across his eyes. Bob is a cat person, not a dog person. Bandit sleeps on the porch in summer and on Bob's lap in winter. As Bob gets sicker, Bandit gravitates closer — finds his lap more often, curls tighter, stays longer. Cats know. By Year 5, Bandit is on Bob's lap every evening, a warm soft weight right when Bob needs one most.Ambient companion NPC — no quest involvement, no interaction mechanic. Bandit exists, purrs, and stays. After Bob's death: sleeps on Bob's empty chair for 3 days, then follows the player to the apiary and sits on the bench next to Bob's hat. Transitions from Bob's companion to the player's. Art assets needed: Cat sprite (idle, sleeping, walking, lap-sitting), porch position, apiary bench position, lap animation for Bob's chair.
Pastor Jim Henning (Methodist Minister)Quiet man who has buried half the town over thirty years. Not a quest-giver — appears only during Bob's memorial arc. Brings a pound cake his wife baked and sits on the porch and says: "I didn't come to talk about God. I came because your uncle built the pews in that church thirty years ago and never set foot inside to sit in them, and I always thought that was the most honest thing I'd ever seen." Bob was not religious. Grief doesn't check your membership card.Single appearance during the post-death memorial sequence. Brief porch scene — no theology, just pastoral care in the small-town idiom. Art assets needed: Pastor Jim NPC sprite, pound cake item.

09b

9b. The Hospice Arc — Bob's Decline and Death

Uncle Bob's story is the emotional spine of Smoke & Honey. His decline from liver failure (a consequence of Agent Orange exposure in Vietnam) unfolds gradually across the five-year arc. This is not a tragedy — it is a good death, anticipated, prepared for, and surrounded by meaning. The hospice philosophy that governs Bob's arc: a good death is not the absence of death but the presence of everything that mattered.

Bob's Health by Year

YearBob's HealthWhat the Player Sees
Year 1Recovering from December episode (jaundice, fluid retention, hospital stay). Fatigued but functional.Bob moves slowly. He sits on the tailgate while the player works. He can still demonstrate techniques but tires quickly. "I'll just watch from here today, kid."
Year 2Stabilized. Good days and bad days. Managing with pills and diet.Bob has more energy some weeks than others. He can walk the apiary but doesn't lift supers anymore. His skin has a yellowish cast that comes and goes. He doesn't discuss it.
Year 3Gradual decline. More bad days than good. VA appointments increase.Bob spends more time in his chair. His hands shake slightly. He defers to the player on physical tasks. His dialogue shifts from teaching to reflecting. "You know what I think about when I sit here? Not the bees. The silence between the bees."
Year 4Significant decline. Hospice considerations. Putting affairs in order.Bob gives the player the workshop key (Q9). Drives to the lawyer (Q10). His dialogue becomes quieter and more deliberate. He is not sad — he is settled. The farewell quests are love disguised as practical business.
Year 5Final decline. Peaceful. Prepared. Surrounded by what mattered.The Rounds (Q11) — Bob visits everyone who mattered. The Last Inspection (Q12) — one more morning in the apiary. Bob dies peacefully at home that night. The community responds. The bees are told. The game continues.

The Death Scene

Bob's death is not shown on-screen as a dramatic event. The player completes The Last Inspection — a warm, quiet afternoon in the apiary together. Bob listens to the player describe the colony. He nods. "Good. That's good." They sit together until the sun gets low. The screen fades. The next morning, the player receives word that Bob passed peacefully in his sleep. No alarm. No emergency. Just the natural end of a life that was ready.

Design Note — Why This Matters: Nathan is a hospice chaplain. Bob is based on Nathan's real uncle — a Vietnam veteran who died of liver failure. This arc must be treated with absolute dignity. Bob's death is not a plot twist or a narrative weapon. It is the fulfillment of a life well-lived and a legacy well-planted. The player doesn't save Bob. They honor him by continuing what he built. Every design decision in this arc should ask: "Does this feel like a good death?" If the answer is no, redesign it.

Family Visits

The player's family visits Cedar Bend periodically — 1-2 events per year starting in Year 2. These are not quests but scripted visit events that provide emotional contrast: a reminder of where the player came from and how far they have traveled.

VisitYearDescription
Parents' First VisitY2 (summer)Parents visit for a weekend. They see the property, the hives, the town. Their dialogue reflects a mix of pride and worry. The player shows them what they have built. Bob sits on the porch and tells them stories about the player they have not heard.
Sibling VisitY3 (summer)A younger sibling visits alone. The player teaches them to hold a smoker, open a hive, and spot the queen. A mirror of Bob teaching the player in Year 1. The sibling asks: "Don't you miss us?" The player's answer is implied by everything the game has shown.
Family HolidayY4 (Kindlemonth)Parents and siblings visit for the holidays. The house is full. Bob is noticeably weaker. The family sees it. Nobody says anything. The player's mother pulls them aside: "He looks thin." The visit ends with the family driving away and the player standing on the porch with Bob. "They're good kids. You were a good kid too. Don't know what happened."
Design Note — Family Visits: These events require family member sprites (parent pair, 1-2 siblings), visit event scripting, family-specific dialogue trees, and guest bedroom art for Bob's house. Family appearances adapt to the character creator — family members share visual traits with the player's chosen appearance. The visits are short (1-2 in-game days) and do not interrupt the mechanical game loop.

After Bob — What Continues

Bob's death is not the end of the game. It is the beginning of the player's full ownership of the life Bob helped them build. After the memorial quests complete:

  • The player inherits the property, all hives, and the workshop (The Deed and the Lawyer).
  • Sandy's smoker becomes the player's primary tool (a permanent gameplay item with a subtle visual glow after the memorial).
  • Bob's chair remains in the apiary. It is never moved. NPCs occasionally comment on it.
  • Bandit the cat sleeps on Bob's empty chair for three days after his death, then follows the player to the apiary and sits on the bench. He stays.
  • The surviving hive — the original hive that started everything — continues producing. It cannot die of natural causes after Bob's death. It is the metaphor made permanent.
  • The game's final narrative beat: the player opens the surviving hive on the first day of the next Quickening. The colony is strong. The queen is laying. The bees are building. They light the smoker — Sandy's smoker, now theirs. They lift the lid. And they get to work.

10

10. Art Direction & Audio

10.0 Game Logo & Brand Mark

Status: APPROVED (March 30, 2026). The official Smoke & Honey game logo has been selected. File: assets/ui/logo/game_logo.png (1200x1200 px, PNG, generated with Leonardo AI Lucid Origin).

CompositionHorizontal split: bee smoker anchored LEFT, game title stacked RIGHT. Smoker occupies roughly 40% of the image width; title fills the remaining 60%.
Smoker designSquat wide silver barrel cylinder body, flat rectangular rigid leather board bellows panels stacked on the left face of the cylinder (like closed book boards with copper rivets), wide dome/cone lid narrowing to a cylindrical tube at the apex. Small side spout nozzle on the right face of the cylinder emits thin wispy smoke curls drifting upward -- smoke comes from the SIDE SPOUT ONLY, not the top opening.
Typography"Smoke" on top line, "&" ampersand in center, "Honey" on bottom line. Ornate amber-orange calligraphic script with cream/off-white highlights, warm brown outline/shadow, and decorative swash flourish beneath "Honey." The "&" is large and stylized.
BackgroundDeep warm amber-brown radial gradient (darker at edges, slightly lighter behind the smoker). No hex drip motifs, no border frames.
Color paletteSmoker: silver-gray body, copper-brown bellows boards, dark copper rivets. Text: cream highlights with amber-gold fill and dark brown shadow. Background: #3a1800 -- #6a3000 range.
UsageTitle screen, Steam capsule art (scaled), itch.io page header, Kickstarter campaign banner, press kit. Do not add drop shadows or color filters when placing on dark backgrounds -- the logo already has its own glow/vignette.
SourceLeonardo AI, Lucid Origin model, 1200x1200 Fast. Research/iteration files in research/art/logos/Almost_There.html.

10.1 Visual Style

Overall StyleWarm, muted, cozy top-down pixel art. Not cartoon-flat, not photorealistic. Think illustrated field guide meets Stardew Valley — pastoral, nostalgic, grounded. Everything feels lit by soft afternoon Iowa sunlight. Nothing is bright or over-saturated.
PerspectiveTrue top-down with a very slight north-tilt (~15°) — the classic SNES/GBC RPG view. You see the roof of buildings from above and the front face below the roofline. This is the Stardew Valley / early Harvest Moon perspective.
Color PaletteEarthy and muted year-round, season-tinted. Base: muted olive-green grass, sandy tan dirt, warm gray stone, dark slate roofs, warm brown timber. Spring tint: soft green + cream. Summer: deep green + amber. Fall: burnt orange + burgundy. Winter: muted blue-gray + warm interior light. No pure saturated primaries.
OutlinesNo pure black (#000000) outlines anywhere. Edges are formed by using the darkest shade of the local hue. This gives the art its soft, painterly quality and is the single most important stylistic rule for new assets.
Hive Frame InspectionSeparate close-up scene — front-facing view (not top-down). Higher detail than the overworld. Readable hexagonal cell grid; color-coded cell states (pale yellow empty comb, golden capped honey, tan capped brood, white larvae, egg dots). Bees have character but are not anthropomorphized. Uses the same warm amber/tan palette family.
EnvironmentApiary grounds show growth over time. Planted trees grow visibly between years. Established apiaries feel lived-in. Ground cover and wildflowers change seasonally. Mowed vs. unmowed grass is visually distinct.
UI PhilosophyAlmost no UI during normal play. The world is the interface. Data surfaces contextually: hover a hive to see a simple status icon; enter inspection mode for detail. UI elements are themed as Langstroth hive frames — thick wooden borders, honeycomb cell interiors, warm amber/beeswax color palette. Panels look like wooden frame components; buttons like individual wax cells; fill bars drain and fill cell-by-cell in amber honey tones. Skeuomorphic but restrained. No harsh beeps or mechanical clicks. See §10.1.4 for the full Langstroth Frame UI Design System specification.
Seasonal TransitionsAnimated transition screen between seasons. Visual change to the apiary environment is gradual week-by-week, not sudden. Palette shift is applied as a screen-level tint layer over the base sprites — one sprite sheet serves all seasons.

Visual Style — Technical Specifications

SpecValue
Base tile size16×16 pixels (matches the Cainos Village asset pack grid)
Small items / inventory icons16×16 pixels
Player character sprite120×120 pixels per frame — 8 directions; idle (1 frame/dir), walk (8 frames/dir), run (8 frames/dir). Single spritesheet 960×2880 px (8 cols × 24 rows). Runtime-loaded via Image.load_from_file(); no Godot import pipeline dependency.
NPC sprites120×120 pixels per frame. 8-direction movement; single spritesheet 960×2880 px per character (8 cols × 24 rows). Runtime-loaded via Image.load_from_file(); no Godot import pipeline dependency. Distinct silhouettes per character.
Building spritesMulti-tile. Small structures ~48–64px wide; larger buildings ~80–96px wide. All rendered at slight north-tilt showing roof (dominant) and front facade (below roofline). Shadow is a separate sprite layer.
World perspectiveTop-down with slight north-tilt. Camera is fixed overhead per scene. Scenes scroll; player walks through them. NOT side-scrolling.
Frame inspection viewFront-facing (portrait orientation). Not top-down. Higher fidelity — individual cells visible at 3–4px per cell. Separate scene loaded on inspect.
Shading depth2–3 tones per color family (mid → light highlight → dark shadow). Dithering used only on ground textures, not characters or buildings.
Shadow systemObjects cast soft drop shadows as a separate CanvasLayer, offset slightly south-east. Shadow color = deep warm brown (#1A0E06) at 25-35% opacity. Never baked into the sprite. Northwest light source, shadows cast southeast. See full shadow system specification in §10.1.5.
Visual referencesPrimary: Pixel Art Top Down – Village by Cainos (the foundation asset pack). Secondary: Stardew Valley (world scale, character charm), early Harvest Moon GBA (palette mood). Hive inspection: field guide illustration style, readable over cozy.

10.1.2 Asset Foundation & Art Direction

The visual foundation of Smoke & Honey is the Pixel Art Top Down – Village asset pack by Cainos (v1.0.7), located in assets/sprites/paid/. This pack provides all core tilesets (grass, dirt, terrain), village buildings, props, plants, and particle effects. Every new asset created for the game — beehives, NPC characters, beekeeping tools, hive frame UI, market stalls — must match this pack's style precisely.

The pack's defining visual characteristics, in order of importance:

  1. No pure black outlines. The single biggest differentiator. All edges use the darkest shade of the local hue. Violating this makes new assets look pasted-in and foreign.
  2. Muted, earthy palette. Olive greens, sandy tans, warm gray stone, dark slate roofs, warm brown timber, muted teal water. No neon. No primaries. The whole scene should feel like one cohesive color family.
  3. 2–3 shade depth per color. Not flat, not over-rendered. Highlight, mid, shadow — that's the full range per color group.
  4. Soft drop shadows as a separate layer. Never baked into the sprite. This allows shadow toggling and consistent lighting across scenes.
  5. Top-down slight north-tilt perspective. Roofs are the dominant face of buildings. The front wall peeks out below the roofline. Ground tiles fill the rest.

Core Palette Reference

RoleDescriptionUse in prompts
Ground grassMuted olive yellow-green, slightly dullmuted olive green ground
Dirt / pathWarm sandy tan, slight orange castsandy tan dirt path
Tree canopy3–4 tones: dark forest green → bright highlight dotrounded fluffy olive-green tree canopy
Building wallCool mid-gray stone, or warm cream plastercool gray stone wall / warm cream plaster wall
Timber framingWarm medium brown, amber highlightswarm brown half-timber frame
RoofDark slate gray, pixel-shaded depthdark slate gray roof with pixel shading
Wood propsTan-to-medium-brown barrels, cratesweathered wood brown
Honey / amberWarm amber gold — #C8860A rangewarm amber honey gold
Beekeeper suitOff-white with warm cast — not pure whiteoff-white warm beekeeping suit

10.1.3 AI Asset Generation — PixelLab Prompt System

For assets not covered by the Cainos pack (beehives, beekeeper character, tools, hive frame UI, NPCs, bee-specific props), the project uses PixelLab.ai as the primary AI generation tool. All new assets must be generated using the master prompt block below as the base, with a subject-specific line appended. The full prompt guide with per-asset templates lives in PIXELLAB_STYLE_GUIDE.md.

Master Prompt Block

Paste this into every PixelLab generation, then add the subject line at the end:

top-down RPG pixel art, 16x16 tile grid, warm muted earthy palette,
olive-green grass, sandy tan dirt paths, half-timbered European village style,
slate gray roofs, warm brown timber framing, stone walls in cool gray,
soft ambient lighting from upper-left, 2-3 shade depth shading,
no hard black outlines (use darkened hue for shadows),
subtle pixel dithering on ground textures, rounded tree canopies
with highlight dots, cozy pastoral mood, Stardew Valley aesthetic,
in the style of Pixel Art Top Down Village by Cainos,
transparent background, [YOUR SUBJECT HERE]

Cedar Bend Diner (building)~80×64 pxCream plaster, red-and-white striped awning, glass front door

Style Rules for New Assets (enforce on every review)

  • No pure black (#000000) outlines — always darkened hue
  • No bright saturated colors — muted and earthy throughout
  • No pure white backgrounds — always transparent PNG
  • No fantasy or medieval theming on BeeKeeper-specific assets — this is rural Iowa
  • No heavy dithering on characters or buildings — dithering is for ground tiles only
  • Shadow is always a separate sprite, never baked in
  • When in doubt: does it look like it belongs in the Cainos Village scene overview screenshot? If yes, ship it. If no, regenerate.

10.1.4 Langstroth Frame UI Design System

All in-game UI elements follow the Langstroth Frame Aesthetic — a unified visual identity that treats every UI component as if it were crafted from the same wooden frames and beeswax comb that the player manages in the hive. This design language was established in the Phase 1 UI sprint and replaces any generic "wood/paper" UI treatment. Every UI asset lives in assets/sprites/ui/; preview composites in assets/sprites/ui/previews/.

Design Language Overview

A Langstroth frame is the rectangular wooden frame that hangs inside a hive box. It has a thick top bar, side bars, and bottom bar forming the frame body, with the interior filled by drawn honeycomb cells (hexagonal wax cells). The UI system translates this into interactive components:

ComponentVisual MetaphorAsset(s)
Panels / menusLangstroth frame with thick wooden border (4px multi-tone), interior wax-cream background with subtle honeycomb cell overlay. Top header band is solid wood with grain lines.menu_panel.png, dialogue_panel.png, panel_wood.png
ButtonsIndividual honeycomb cell or small wooden frame segment. Normal: wax-cream interior with honeycomb dot pattern. Hover: amber-washed interior, brightened. Pressed: inverted shadow (sunken), darker amber fill — like a capped cell.btn_normal.png, btn_hover.png, btn_pressed.png
Fill bars (XP, energy, honey)A row of comb cells set into a wooden rail. Cells fill left-to-right with warm amber honey. Divider lines every cell-width. Background cells show empty-comb cream; filled cells show amber gold with highlight. No gradients — discrete cell fills only.xp_bar.png, xp_bar_bg.png, xp_bar_fill.png, energy_bar_bg.png, energy_bar_fill.png
Toast notificationsSticky note pinned to a frame — warm cream parchment body, amber top "tape" strip, torn left edge suggesting the note is pinned or peeled from the frame. Stack vertically from the top-right.notification_bg.png
Dialogue boxesField inspection card / beekeeper's notebook page. Thick wooden outer frame border, solid wood top header bar (with grain lines), warm wax-paper interior with faint ruled lines. Portrait area inset on the left with its own wooden sub-frame.dialogue_panel.png
Speech bubblesWooden-framed box with a triangular wooden tail pointing toward the speaker. Interior is wax-paper white with a very faint honeycomb overlay. Top edge is a thin header bar.speech_bubble.png
Title plateRectangular wooden frame with an amber/honey recess panel inside. The amber recess is where the game title or panel title sits. Four corner nail markers at the frame joints.title_plate.png
HUD barsLangstroth top bar and bottom bar — thick horizontal wood rails across the full viewport width. Top bar: grain lines run horizontal; amber resin spots for texture. Bottom bar: lighter top face, deeper shadow bottom. Both are 16–20px tall.hud_top_bar.png, hud_bottom_bar.png
Interact promptSmall wooden tag with a punch-hole on the left (frame hanging notch). Wax-cream interior. Used for contextual "Press [E]" prompts.interact_prompt_bg.png
Inventory slotsHexagonally-influenced square cell. Wood-framed edges with an empty-comb interior. Cross-hair dividers suggest the comb grid. New-item variant glows amber.inventory_slot.png, inventory_slot_new.png
Log pageWarm parchment tile with ruled lines and a corner fold. Used as the background texture for the Knowledge Log and field notebooks. The margin line at left is a warm amber rule.log_page.png
Map backgroundParchment interior with aged texture (light dithered dots), enclosed in a 6px Langstroth wood border with grain lines and corner joint marks. Faint grid overlay.map_background.png
Level badgeHexagonal badge — the natural beehive hex. Wood outer ring, amber interior, bright honey center highlight. Used for the player level number display.level_badge.png

Frame Border Construction Rules

All panels use a consistent multi-layer border system that replicates the cross-section of a wooden Langstroth frame:

  1. Layer 0 (outermost): WD_DARKEST — 1px outer shadow/ground shadow edge
  2. Layer 1: WD_MID — main wood body fill
  3. Layer 2: WD_LIGHT — inner highlight edge (lighter face of the wood)
  4. Layer 3: WD_MID — secondary body layer
  5. Layer 4+ (interior): WAX_WHITE — interior content area

Corner joints are marked with a small 5×5px WD_DARKEST dot — representing the nail or screw at each frame joint. Top header bars within panels are 12–16px solid WD_MID with horizontal grain lines at 4px intervals.

Honeycomb Cell Grid — Usage Rules

  • Cell size in fill bars: 8–10px wide, 6–8px tall (landscape hex approximation)
  • Cell dividers: 1px lines in CELL_WALL at regular intervals across fill bars
  • Interior panel honeycomb overlay: very subtle, alpha ≤30%, used as texture not pattern
  • Cell walls must never dominate the readability of text — keep overlay opacity low
  • Fill bars always fill left-to-right in discrete cell segments — no fractional cell fills (snap to nearest whole cell)

Button Cell Design — Three States

StateInterior FillTop EdgeBottom EdgeMetaphor
NormalWAX_CREAM with CELL_WALL honeycomb dotsWD_LIGHT (lit from above)WD_DARKEST (shadow)Drawn but empty comb cell
HoverHONEY_PALE with stronger dot patternWD_PALE (brighter)WD_DARKCell filling with fresh nectar
PressedCELL_HONEY with AMBER_DARK dotsWD_DARKEST (inverted — sunken)WD_LIGHT (inverted)Capped, filled cell — pressed in

Fill Bar Animation Spec — Cell-by-Cell Fill

Fill bars (XP, energy, honey) do not animate as smooth gradients. They fill in discrete cell segments — the bar visually fills one cell at a time as the underlying value increases. This gives a satisfying, organic quality consistent with the honeycomb metaphor:

  • Each cell segment transitions from CELL_EMPTY → AMBER_MID as it fills
  • The currently-filling cell shows a partial amber overlay scaled to the fractional fill within that cell
  • On level-up or full fill, a brief pulse animation flashes each cell in sequence from left to right (AMBER_LIGHT pulse, 3 frames)
  • Energy drain plays in reverse — rightmost cell empties first

Font and Text Treatment

  • All UI text renders on WAX_WHITE or WAX_CREAM backgrounds
  • Primary label color: WD_DARKEST (38, 18, 6) — warm very-dark-brown, not black
  • Accent labels (values, quantities): AMBER_DARK
  • Header text in wood bars: WAX_CREAM
  • Preferred pixel font: any slightly condensed, warm-toned pixel bitmap font. Avoid pure-white or pure-black text.

PixelLab Prompt — UI Elements (Langstroth Frame Theme)

Use this prompt base for generating any new UI element assets to match the Langstroth frame aesthetic:

pixel art UI element, game interface sprite, Langstroth hive frame aesthetic,
thick wooden frame border (warm brown #6C4016, dark edge #46260C),
interior filled with warm wax-cream color (#FCF5D7) or honeycomb cell pattern,
warm amber honey tones (#C8820C, #E6A220), beeswax cream background,
hexagonal comb cells where appropriate, aged wood grain detail,
no pure black outlines (darkest tone #261206), no gradients (dithering and cell patterns only),
no bright saturated colors, warm muted earthy palette,
transparent background where applicable, [YOUR UI ELEMENT HERE]

10.1.5 Shadow System (APPROVED)

Shadows ground every object in the world and separate the game from looking "flat." All shadows use a consistent northwest light source (shadows cast southeast) and are rendered on a separate CanvasLayer below character/object sprites. Shadow color is deep warm brown (#1A0E06) at 25-35% opacity — never pure black.

Shadow AssetTypeSizePhaseNotes
Character blob shadow (universal)Sprite12x6pxPhase 1Single elliptical asset reused for all characters (player + NPCs). Scales slightly with movement.
Hive ground shadow (Langstroth)Sprite28x12pxPhase 1Already in registry — confirm delivery.
Hive ground shadow (Warre)Sprite24x10pxPhase 2Narrower, taller hive = different shadow shape.
Building shadow atlas (14 buildings)Sprite sheetvariesPhase 1-31 per building, matched to building phase. Pre-baked, warm-toned transparency.
Tree shadow — round canopy (oak, maple, generic)Sprite48x32pxPhase 2Reusable across round-canopy trees. Organic dappled edges.
Tree shadow — wide canopy (linden, cottonwood)Sprite64x40pxPhase 2Broader spread pattern.
Tree shadow — orchard (apple, cherry, pear, plum)Sprite40x28pxPhase 2Smaller, grouped spacing for orchard layout.
Tree shadow — weeping (willow)Sprite56x48pxPhase 2Long trailing drape pattern; unique to willow.
Tree shadow — bare/winter (universal)Sprite32x24pxPhase 3Thin branch-only shadow for leafless trees.
Prop shadow atlas (8 generic shapes)Sprite sheetvariesPhase 1Reusable: small rect, medium rect, long thin, circular, etc.
Fence line shadowTileable16x4pxPhase 1Repeating shadow for picket/post fences.
Interior object shadows (soft contact)Sprite setvariesPhase 36-8 generic shapes for furniture/equipment.
Design Note — Shadow Implementation: Shadows are implemented as a shadow CanvasLayer (z-index below characters). Character blob shadows scale slightly during movement. Building and tree shadows shift subtly with time-of-day (morning = long shadows east, midday = short, evening = long shadows west). The prop shadow atlas is a reusable set of generic shapes rather than per-prop custom sprites. Total shadow assets: 12 unique sprites/sheets covering all object types.

10.1.6 Lighting System (APPROVED)

Lighting creates mood, marks time passing, and makes the player feel the season. The approach is layered color overlays and point light sprites rather than complex real-time lighting.

Time-of-Day Color Grading

The entire overworld gets a full-screen CanvasModulate color overlay that interpolates smoothly through the day cycle. The artist provides reference palette cards for each time period.

Time PeriodDescriptionColorModulate %
Dawn (5-7am)Warm pink-gold wash. Soft, diffused. Dew sparkle active.#FFE8C015%
Morning (7-10am)Clean, bright. Gentle warm yellow. Bees most active.#FFF5E08%
Midday (10am-2pm)Neutral / very slight warm. Shadows at shortest.Base palette0% (no modulate)
Afternoon (2-5pm)Golden hour begins. Warm amber creep.#FFD89010%
Evening (5-8pm)Deep golden-orange. Long shadows. Fireflies appear.#FFC06020%
Dusk (8-9pm)Purple-blue creep at edges, warm center.#C8A0FF + #FFB04012% + 8%
Night (9pm-5am)Deep blue wash. Moon-lit. Only windows and lamp posts glow.#4060A035%
Point Light Sprites

Warm light sources that punch through the night/dusk color grading. Each light is a radial gradient sprite rendered additively on a light layer, creating pools of warmth around windows, lamp posts, and the player's lantern.

Seasonal Color Palettes

Each season shifts the base world palette subtly (separate from time-of-day). Spring: fresh greens, soft pastels, cool undertones in shadows. Summer: warm, saturated, golden-green grass. Fall: amber, burnt orange, russet, desaturated greens shift to gold. Winter: cool blue-gray, muted, high contrast between snow white and dark branches. The artist provides 4 seasonal palette reference cards that guide all environmental art.

Interior Lighting

Interior scenes (house, diner, stores, post office, Honey House) are not affected by the outdoor time-of-day system. Each interior gets a baked ambient light layer with warm pools around light fixtures and slightly darker corners. Window daylight spill rectangles shift with outdoor time-of-day (4 variants per window).

Lighting AssetTypeSizePhaseNotes
Seasonal palette reference cards (4)ReferencedocPhase 1Guides all environmental art from day one.
